href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D23853%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJiaxing%2BXiang%252C%2BLuxiang%2BMa%252C%2BYanxia%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D40%26issueNum%3D45%26contentID%3Dacs.langmuir.4c02868%26title%3DBall-Milling-Assisted%2BN%252FO%2BCodoping%2Bfor%2BEnhanced%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BCoconut-Shell-Derived%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%2Bin%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0743-7463%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dlangd5%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D23863%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2024" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/medium/la4c02868_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as cost-effective alternatives or competitors to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage applications. However, their development has been hindered by the high cost of hard carbon (HC) anodes and poor electrochemical performance. To enhance the sodium storage capacity and rate performance of HC, this study accelerated the electrochemical performance of coconut-shell-derived HC anodes for SIBs through N/O codoping using ball milling and pyrolysis. Experimental results demonstrate that the simultaneous introduction of N and O generates a synergistic effect, increasing the surface oxygen-containing functional groups, defects, and interlayer spacing of coconut-shell-derived HC through the codoping of light elements. The excellent strategy has increased the slope capacity and platform capacity of HC, and the synergistic modification of N/O has increased its reversible specific capacity from 272 to 343 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), with a retention rate of approximately 92.1% after 100 cycles. In addition, it also exhibits an excellent rate performance, reaching 178 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In summary, this study presents an effective strategy for modifying biomass-derived HC.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D23853%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJiaxing%2BXiang%252C%2BLuxiang%2BMa%252C%2BYanxia%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D40%26issueNum%3D45%26contentID%3Dacs.langmuir.4c02868%26title%3DBall-Milling-Assisted%2BN%252FO%2BCodoping%2Bfor%2BEnhanced%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BCoconut-Shell-Derived%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%2Bin%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0743-7463%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dlangd5%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D23863%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291356&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Defects" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Defects" class="article__tags__link">Defects</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290865&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nitrogen" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nitrogen" class="article__tags__link">Nitrogen</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292534&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxygen" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxygen" class="article__tags__link">Oxygen</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The increasing demand for efficient and sustainable energy storage solutions has driven extensive research, especially in the field of rechargeable batteries. In particular, the integration of renewable energy sources such as solar and wind power into the electrical grid has underscored the need for reliable and cost-effective energy storage technologies. Due to the prominent abundance of sodium in the earth, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as one of the most promising alternatives for large-scale energy storage. Anode materials are widely believed to significantly influence the overall performance of SIBs. Consequently, there has been a surge of interest in the development of various types of anode materials. Carbon-based materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> alloy materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> and organic compounds <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 7 to 10">(7−10)</a> among others have been investigated as potential anode candidates for SIBs. Biomass-derived hard carbon (HC), characterized by its disordered structure composed of curved graphite-like microcrystals, stacked nanopores, and defects, has garnered significant attention as a viable anode material for SIBs. Hu <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> reported the development of HC derived from corn cobs, exhibiting a reversible specific capacity of 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> through simple treatment and carbonization. Li <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> synthesized HC by pyrolysis cotton, which has a natural tubular structure and a capacity of 315 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Its higher capacity is achieved through defect adsorption, surface functional group reactions, interlayer intercalation, and nanopore filling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> The charging/discharging curve of HC mainly includes the slope area and the platform area, corresponding to potentials below 0.1 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and above 0.1 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> However, due to the lack of sufficient sodium storage sites and conductivity in some precursors, their capacity and rate performance are poor. In addition, although some companies can prepare high-performance HC, the manufacturing process is complex, and the cost is high. All of the above issues are significantly behind large-scale applications of SIBs for energy storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">Due to the direct influence of the microstructure on the sodium storage capacity of HC, changing the microstructure and enhancing conductivity through simple processes should effectively improve the above issues. Usually, introducing N atoms can create defects, enhance conductivity, and improve its sodium storage capacity and rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Hu <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> prepared nitrogen-doped sample N-HC, and due to its effect of increasing defects and expanding interlayer spacing, its reversible capacity increased by 154.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> compared to the undoped sample; however, excessive defects result in only about 50% of its first cycle coulombic efficiency (FCCE). Huang <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> used pyrrole monomer as carbon and nitrogen sources to prepare nitrogen-doped carbon fibers, which improved the electrical conductivity of the material; the prepared carbon anode exhibits a high specific capacity of 73 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Subsequently, there have been numerous reports on the use of NH<sub>3</sub> treatment, plasma treatment, chemical vapor deposition, and other methods to doping nitrogen to cause defects or increase interlayer spacing to improve sodium storage capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20−23)</a> Moreover, O atom doping of the oxygen atoms can form oxygen functional groups on the surface of HC, and the reaction between oxygen functional groups and Na<sup>+</sup> helps to increase the Na<sup>+</sup> slope capacity. Wu <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> prepared coal-driven HC with a C═O content of 26.9% through preoxidation, resulting in rapid Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics and a significant increase in reversible capacity to 306 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. These two strategies can not only increase sodium storage sites but also improve conductivity. However, N element doping has the disadvantage of reducing FCCE, while the O element doping has a weak effect on improving conductivity. Therefore, developing a processing technology that can improve sodium storage sites and conductivity without reducing FCCE is crucial for accelerating the large-scale commercialization of SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we use a simple process to increase the sodium storage sites and conductivity of HC by simultaneously doping N and O with a single doping source. Low-cost urea has high nitrogen content (25%) and oxygen content (16%) as the nitrogen source and oxygen source to configure high content N/O-doped coconut-shell-derived HC (N/O-HC) anodes for SIBs. The synergistic effect of oxygen and nitrogen increases both the platform capacity and slope capacity and exhibits rapid reaction kinetics. The treated sample N/O-HC anode has a higher reversible specific capacity than the untreated sample coconut-derived hard carbon (C-HC), with a capacity increase of up to 71 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and excellent rate performance, reaching 178 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, with almost no decrease in FCCE. In summary, the simple dual-element introduction modification strategy provides a reference for biomass-based modification.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Preparation of N/O-HC Samples</h3><div class="NLM_p">All chemical reagents used were of analytical grade and employed directly without further purification. The N/O-HC was typically prepared through a combination of ball milling and pyrolysis, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. Initially, coconut shells purchased from Landarun were calcined at 1300 °C for 2 h in a nitrogen (N<sub>2</sub>) atmosphere to obtain C-HC. Subsequently, the C-HC powder was mixed thoroughly with urea at weight ratios of 1:1, 1:2, and 1:3 by ball milling at 300 rpm for 5 h, respectively. Following this, the mixtures were calcined at 300 °C for 2 h in an argon (Ar) atmosphere using a tube-type furnace, with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. The resulting specimens were designated as N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3, respectively.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/medium/la4c02868_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagram of preparation of N/O-HC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Assembling of a Half SIB</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Both nitrogen-doped and undoped HC were mixed with Super P carbon black and poly(vinylidene fluoride) in a weight ratio of 8:1:1 in <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidinone to form a uniformly mixed anode slurry. After magnetic stirring, the resulting slurries were coated onto a well-cleaned copper foil using an H-coating machine. Following the coating process, the anode copper foil was dried at 120 °C overnight in a vacuum oven (DZF-6051). Subsequently, the coated anode films were punched into disks with a diameter of 12 mm. The average loading capacity of active substances is 2.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. These anode discs, along with fiberglass and sodium foil, were utilized to assemble CR 2032 coin-type SIBs with the anode acting as the working electrode, fiberglass as the separator, and sodium foil as the cathode. The assembly process was conducted within a glovebox filled with argon (Ar) (Universal 2400/750/900, Shanghai Mikrouna Mech Tech Co., Ltd.). To connect the electrodes, 0.1 mL of dimethoxyethane containing 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt was used as the electrolyte. For comparative analysis, in addition to N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3, C-HC was also employed to examine the effects of nitrogen and oxygen atom doping.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performance of assembled half cells using different working electrodes, including C-HC (coconut HC), N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3, was evaluated through galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling and cyclic voltammetry (CV). Specifically, CV curves were recorded between 0 and 1.5 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) using an electrochemical workstation [Autolab electrochemical workstation (MULTI AUTOLAB M204)]. The scanning rates were set at 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, and 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were performed by using a Landt CT2001A system in the voltage range of 0.01–3 V at room temperature.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Materials morphology evolution characteristics were observed by field emission-scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM, ZEISS Sigma 300) equipped with an energy-dispersive spectroscope (EDS), transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL JEM F200), and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM, Thermo Fisher Talos F200s). N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherms were carried out at 77 K by a N<sub>2</sub> adsorption analyzer (NOVA 2200e, Quantachrome Instruments). Before the measurement, all samples were outgassed at 180 °C overnight. Specific surface areas of specimens were calculated using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method. Pore size distributions were obtained from the adsorption branch of the isotherm using the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method. Structures and components of the materials were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD) with Cu Kα radiation (XRD, DX-2700) and Raman spectroscopy (Renishaw inVia). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were conducted on an ESCALAB 250 spectrometer (Thermo Scientific) with the mono Kα.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Morphology and Structure Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and microstructure of the as-prepared samples are characterized by using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and HRTEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a–f). The C-HC sample was synthesized through pyrolysis of a coconut shell. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the C-HC sample is a large and dense block-like substance (approximately 8 μm in diameter). In contrast, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b reveals that the N/O-HC-2 sample has smaller particles, a result of ball milling. Additional morphology and structural information is visible in the TEM and HRTEM images of C-HC and N/O-HC-2 samples. The C-HC sample exhibits long locally ordered crystal domains, extending up to 20 nm in length, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d. Conversely, TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e,f) show the highly disordered lattice of the N/O-HC-2 sample, characterized by short-range ordered and intricate vortex structures, leading to the formation of more pores. This structure is conducive to the storage of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> In addition, in N/O-HC-2, multiple double-layer interlayer distances of graphite nanocrystals were selected and the average interlayer distance was calculated to be 0.39 nm, which is greater than that of C-HC (0.38 nm). The local expansion of local interlayer distance in N/O-HC-2 may result from “introduction of nitrogen element”. A larger interlayer spacing can effectively reduce the dynamic hindrance during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, thereby increasing the Na<sup>+</sup> of storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> To verify the differences in lattice structures between the N/O-HC-2 and C-HC samples, we conducted XRD and Raman spectroscopy analyses. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g, the XRD pattern of the C-HC sample shows two peaks at 2θ = 23.5° and 43.5°, corresponding to the (002) and (100) lattice planes of graphite. The broad, low-intensity peaks indicate a low degree of graphitization. In contrast, the N/O-HC-2 sample exhibits less graphitization than the C-HC sample. The (100) lattice plane of the N/O-HC-2 sample is almost nonexistent for the C-HC sample, which is in accordance with the highly disordered structure of the N/O-HC-2 sample observed in the HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f). Furthermore, it is notable that the (002) diffraction peak of the N/O-HC-2 sample shifts to a lower diffraction angle (2θ = 22.6°) compared with those of the C-HC (2θ = 23.5°) samples. According to Bragg’s law, the (002) plane interlayer distance of the N/O-HC-2 sample can be calculated to be 0.39 nm, which is larger than that of the HC (0.38 nm). This is in agreement with the HRTEM results (0.39 and 0.38 nm for N/O-HC-2 and C-HC, respectively) shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d–f.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/medium/la4c02868_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Characterization of C-HC and N/O-HC materials. (a) SEM images of C-HC; (b) SEM images of N/O-HC-2; (c,d) TEM and HRTEM images of C-HC; (e,f) TEM and HRTEM images of N/O-HC-2 (inset in HRTEM images: corresponding intensity profile for the line scan across the lattice fringes); (g) XRD patterns of C-HC and N/O-HC-2; (h) Raman spectra of C-HC and N/O-HC-2; TEM image and EDS elemental mappings of (i,j) C-HC and (k,l) N/O-HC-2.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Raman spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h) reveals that the intensity ratio of the D band to the G band (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) is higher for the N/O-HC-1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>, 1.24), N/O-HC-2 (1.27), and N/O-HC-3 sample (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>, 1.36) than for the C-HC sample (1.12). The D band at 1367 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the G band at 1577 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to defect-induced activation of the C<sub>6</sub> ring A<sub>1g</sub> breathing vibration and sp<sup>2</sup>-bonded carbon E<sub>2g</sub> vibration, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> However, excessive disorder can damage the structural integrity of the carbon skeleton, and excessive defects may lead to increased capacity but decreased FCCE. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The higher intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of the D band to the G band further confirms that the structure of the N/O-HC samples is more disordered compared to that of the C-HC sample. This is consistent with HRTEM and XRD results. It should be noted that the HRTEM results can only display partial microcrystalline spacing, so the subsequent mechanism discussion will mainly focus on XRD calculations.</div><div class="NLM_p">Specifically, the carbon oxygen nitrogen mapping maps of C-HC samples corresponding to the TEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i,j reveal a uniform distribution of carbon and oxygen atoms in the C-HC sample, with nitrogen elements being almost absent. In contrast, for the N/O-HC-2 sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>k,l), nitrogen elements are uniformly distributed alongside carbon and oxygen elements, forming a homogeneous chemical phase. The nitrogen component is evenly dispersed and incorporated into the carbon lattice. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> This signifies the successful synthesis of N/O-doped HC materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">The pore size distribution has a significant impact on the performance of HC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a> displays the N<sub>2</sub> physical adsorption isotherms and BJH pore size distribution for C-HC, N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3 samples. All samples exhibit typical type II adsorption isotherms. The pore volume of nitrogen-doped samples is significantly higher than that of the C-HC samples. Notably, the N/O-HC-2 sample shows a significantly higher pore volume compared to the C-HC sample, which is consistent with the observations from TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f). BJH analysis reveals that the most prominent feature of the pore size distribution for N/O-HC-2 is the presence of micropores with sizes around 3 nm, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>. According to the BET method, the specific surface area of the N/O-HC-2 sample is calculated to be 24.52 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, with a total pore volume of 0.04 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, the C-HC sample has a specific surface area of 8.09 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and a total pore volume of 0.01 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, consistent with the observations from the SEM, TEM, and HRTEM images. These results further confirm that nitrogen doping with urea leads to the formation of increased micropores and mesopores, which is beneficial for improving sodium storage performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">XPS was further used to analyze the surface elemental composition and bonding configurations of the synthesized C-HC, N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3 samples. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a–c, the XPS results of C-HC show C 1s and O 1s bonding configurations; no N 1s spectral peak is detected in the XPS survey spectrum, and the content of the elemental O is 4.48% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). In comparison, the XPS results of N/O-HC-1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>), N/O-HC-2 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), and N/O-HC-3 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>) show the binding energies of the three main peaks corresponding to C 1s, N 1s, and O 1s which are located at 285.0, 400.0, and 532.0 eV, respectively. XPS shows that the N element content in samples N/O-HC-1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), N/O-HC-2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), and N/O-HC-3 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>) is 3.36%, 4.65%, and 18.93%, respectively. Excessive nitrogen doping can detrimentally affect the carbon framework, leading to reduced conductivity, increased impedance, and compromised cycling stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Notably, the oxygen content is measured to be 6.78%, 12.57%, and 9.33%, respectively. The diminished oxygen presence in N/O-HC-3 suggests that an overabundance of nitrogen generates excessive defects, thereby reducing potential oxygen reaction sites. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Overall, the incorporation of oxygen and nitrogen is dictated by the urea proportion added to the mixture. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3b</a>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4b</a>, the C 1s spectrum comprises a combination of three peaks at 284.7, 285.7, and 288.8 eV, representing sp<sup>2</sup> C–C, C–N, and O–C═O, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–S3</a>). In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3c</a>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4c</a>, the O 1s spectrum comprises a combination of three peaks at 531.0, 532.3, and 533.4 eV, representing sp<sup>2</sup> C═O, O–C–O, and HO–C═O, respectively. Among these functional groups, the presence of C–OH is particularly important because the hydroxyl groups improve the surface wettability. It is worth noting that the increase in the amount of C═O functional groups is significant, C═O + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>–</sup> → C–O–Na reaction for contributing sodium storage capacity, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> which is caused by the ball milling of urea and C-HC followed by pyrolysis. The N 1s spectrum can be divided into two peaks located at 398.5 and 400.1 eV, corresponding to pyridinic N and pyrrolic N, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3d</a>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4d</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–S3</a>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/medium/la4c02868_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Comprehensive characterization of N/O-HC-2 and C-HC. (a) Total XPS spectrum and (b) C 1s and (c) O 1s spectra and (d) N 1s of N/O-HC-2; (e) total XPS spectrum and (f) C 1s and (g) O 1s spectra of C-HC; (h) proposed distribution of nitrogen species in N/O-HC series samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Furthermore, the higher intensity of the pyridinic N peak in the N 1s spectrum suggests that nitrogen in the N/O-HC samples primarily exists in the pyridinic N state (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h), and as shown in the figure, the formation of pyridine nitrogen will produce a defect, which has been shown to be more favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> storage and reversible capacity in materials. In theory, each pyridinic N contributes one π electron to the graphite π system, thereby enhancing the conductivity of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> In short, the introduction of appropriate N and O atoms is beneficial for storing Na<sup>+</sup> through the pseudocapacitive effect and contributing to the slope capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> In this study, nitrogen/oxygen codoped samples (N/O-HC) were obtained solely through co-pyrolysis of urea and C-HC precursors. In this process, mechanical ball milling also plays an important role; a comparison was made with the high nitrogen/oxygen content of N/O-HC-2 (4.65% and 12.57%). In contrast, the F–N/O-HC-2 mixed sample, prepared using manual hand milling, shows nitrogen and oxygen contents of 1.42% and 14.01%, respectively, as depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5 and Table S5</a> according to XPS analysis. Thus, employing urea in combination with ball milling and Ar pyrolysis represents an excellent strategy for producing nitrogen/oxygen-doped carbon materials with the potential for high performance in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">To investigate and compare the electrochemical performances of C-HC and N/O-HC (N/O-HC-<i>X</i>, where <i>X</i> represents the urea-to-precursor ratio) samples as potential anode materials for SIBs, half coin cells were assembled with sodium metal as the counter electrode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a-d displays the first two CV cycles of C-HC and N/O-HC (various doping levels) samples between 0 and 1.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. In the first cycle, irreversible cathodic peaks are observed at 0.15 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, corresponding to electrolyte decomposition, followed by the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Notably, it should be mentioned that N/O-HC samples exhibit cathodic and anodic peaks around 0.1 V, which is evidence reported by other researchers, indicating the presence of redox reactions of SIB carbon anodes at low potentials. The cathodic and anodic peaks around 0.1 V are attributed to the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> on graphene-like planes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> As the area within the CV curve increases with a higher proportion of urea, this suggests that the capacity will correspondingly increase. However, the excessive irreversible cathodic peak observed in N/O-HC-3 indicates significant SEI film formation, leading to extensive irreversible sodiation, which could potentially decrease the FCCE. The galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the samples before and after doping are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e–h, and they are in good agreement with the CV data. The results indicate that at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the first reversible capacities of N/O-HC (N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3) are significantly higher than that of C-HC, with values of 296, 343, and 356 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, compared to C-HC’s 272 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The capacity increases with a higher urea ratio, consistent with previous predictions. Notably, N/O-HC-3 exhibits the highest capacity; however, its plateau capacity (depicted in gray) diminishes, while its slope capacity (depicted in blue) augments. This shift may lead to an elevated median voltage, consequently reducing the overall voltage range and energy density of the full battery. The FCCEs for N/O-HC (N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3) are slightly lower than that of C-HC, with values of 68.0%, 69.8%, and 66.1% compared to HC’s 71.4%. The higher ICE of the raw material C-HC is due to fewer defects and less irreversible sodiation. It is worth noting that ICE has been reduced but does not significantly decrease the energy density, as the increased capacity of doped samples can offset this minor effect. This may be attributed to the presence of more defects and oxygen functional groups that consume more Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Among the samples, N/O-HC-2 demonstrates the highest FCCE. Conversely, the notably reduced FCCE of N/O-HC-3 aligns with CV analysis predictions. This decline could be attributed to an excess of nitrogen incorporation, which leads to an abundance of defects and consequently increases irreversible sodium insertion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i) was measured for C-HC, N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3 samples. The spectra both before and after doping show a compressed semicircle in the high-frequency region and a straight line in the low-frequency region. The semicircle in the equivalent circuit shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i represents the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), reflecting charge transfer at the electrode–electrolyte interface. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value for N/O-HC-2 is significantly lower than that of C-HC, indicating the enhanced conductivity of N/O-HC-2 within the cell. However, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of N-HC-3 sample increased by nearly twice compared to C-HC. The increase in impedance is attributed to a higher nitrogen content leading to a higher amount of pyridine nitrogen, resulting in excessive defects [<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values are the highest (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>)], which in turn leads to a decrease in the conductivity of the carbon skeleton, which is greater than its electron contribution of <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> which infers that its rate performance will deteriorate.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/medium/la4c02868_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Cyclic voltammograms of (a) C-HC, (b) N/O-HC-1, (c) N/O-HC-2, and (d) N/O-HC-3 at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of (e) C-HC, (f) N/O-HC-1, (g) N/O-HC-2, and (h) N/O-HC-3 at a current density of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (i) EIS of C-HC, N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3; CV curves of (j) C-HC and (k) N/O-HC-2 at scan rates of 0.1–0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (l) <i>b</i>-value analysis using the relationship between the peak currents and the square root of scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The N/O-HC-2 obtained by regulating the urea ratio is expected to be further applied due to its high capacity and minimal impedance. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>j,k displays the CV curves of C-HC and N/O-HC-2 samples at different scan rates. Near 0.1 V, the oxidation–reduction peaks of N/O-HC-2 exhibit higher intensities compared to C-HC, indicating that N/O-HC-2 provides a higher platform capacity through intercalation. Furthermore, the relationship between the peak current (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>) and the square root of the scan rate (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> <i>s</i><sup>–1/2</sup>) is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>l. Both C-HC and N/O-HC-2 exhibit excellent linear behavior, conforming to the Randles–Sevcik relationship. By calculation, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of N/O-HC-2 is determined to be 4.81 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is larger than that of C-HC (2.58 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). The above results indicate that the rate performance of N/O-HC-2 is better. Overall, the number of defects is crucial for regulating the capacity of slopes and platforms.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further assess the impact of hierarchical porosity and N and O doping on the electrochemical properties of the carbon materials, capacity, rate performance, and cycling stability tests were conducted through galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>). As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, all N/O-HC samples exhibit higher capacities than the C-HC samples at both current densities of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The reversible specific capacities after doping for N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3 are 296, 343, and 356 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which are significantly higher than the 272 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> of the C-HC sample. Furthermore, the working voltage of N/O-HC is slightly higher than that of the C-HC samples. To characterize the working voltage, the average midvoltage (voltage at which half of the discharge capacity is reached) was calculated for N/O-HC and C-HC samples during 100 cycles at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The average midvoltages for C-HC, N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3 are 0.053, 0.074, 0.058, and 0.062 V, respectively. Although the slightly higher working voltage of N/O-HC may to some extent reduce the overall cell’s working voltage, the high capacity of N/O-HC compensates for this gap, ensuring that the full cell energy does not decrease. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b provides a further analysis of the capacity contributions. The main capacity increase of N/O-HC samples is contributed by the slope. The sloping capacity (&gt;0.1 V) of N/O-HC samples is increased by around 50 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The slope capacity increases with the increase of urea ratio, which is essentially due to the increase of defects and oxygen groups, thereby increasing the sodium storage active sites. N/O-HC-2 shows the most significant increase in plateau capacity (&lt;0.1 V), while N/O-HC-3 exhibits a decrease of 19 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. This indicates that excessive nitrogen doping will reduce the use of graphite microcrystals as active sites for low-pressure platform sodium storage. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c presents a comparative analysis of rate performance among N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, N/O-HC-3, and C-HC samples across varying current densities. From a current density of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> increasing to 60, 150, 300, 600, 900, and 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, N/O-HC-2 consistently outperforms, maintaining reversible specific capacities of 338, 307, 289, 272, 250, 222, and 178 mA h<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Nevertheless, the overall performance of the N/O-HC series exceeds that of the C-HC samples. The superior rate performance of N/O-HC-2 is attributed to its low impedance and high ionic diffusivity, essentially stemming from an optimal incorporation of nitrogen and oxygen that enhances the material’s conductivity. Conversely, the poor rate capability of N/O-HC-3 aligns with previous impedance speculations, indicating that the improved electronic conductivity due to excess nitrogen doping is insufficient to counteract the reduced conductivity caused by carbon framework disruptions and the creation of carbon vacancies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Therefore, the excessive introduction of heteroatoms is not conducive to improving the rate performance of the battery. More importantly, N/O-HC-2 achieves better performance than many previous studies of biomass-based HC materials (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d,e). Regarding cycling stability, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f, at a current density of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the N/O-HC-2 sample achieves a discharge capacity of 315.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, significantly higher than the C-HC sample (262.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), with 92.1% capacity retention rate. Long cycling life is also crucial for practical applications in SIBs. To study the long-term performance of the N/O-HC samples for SIBs, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g, cycling was performed at a high current density (600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). After 300 cycles, a reversible discharge capacity of 199 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained, and the capacity retention rate was 82.8% compared to the third cycle. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> shows the cycling process of different samples in the first 100 cycles, where N/O-HC-2 exhibits the best stability, while N/O-HC-3 has a higher initial capacity but the cycling decay is very fast, which is consistent with the previous analysis.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/medium/la4c02868_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance of C-HC and N/O-HC (N/O-HC-1, N/O-HC-2, and N/O-HC-3): (a) initial charge–discharge curves at current densities of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (b) comparison of capacity of first charge at current densities of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate capacity at different current densities from 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (d) rate performance of N/O-HC-2 samples; (e) comparison of ratio and capacity of biomass-based derived HC; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 11, 12 and 38 to 43">(11,12,38−43)</a> (f) cycling performance at current densities of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (g) cycling performance of N/O-HC-2 at current densities of 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to comprehensively evaluate the performance of materials in ester-based electrolytes, this study conducted comparative tests using an electrolyte (1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DMC = 1:1 vol %). Compared with the ether-based electrolyte (343 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), the N/O-HC-2 sample has a reversible specific capacity of 302 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the ester-based electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>). The rate performance shows that it has a better high rate performance in ether-based electrolytes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>), attributed to its superior electronic conductivity. The long cycle test (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6c</a>) shows that its cycling stability is better and more stable in ether-based electrolytes. Overall, N/O-HC-2 has better compatibility with ether-based electrolytes. Although HC may limit the positive electrode voltage when using ether-based electrolytes in full batteries, it is believed that this issue will be resolved in the near future.</div><div class="NLM_p last"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a> investigates the capacity, rate, FCCE, and cycling performance of biomass-based HC for SIBs in recent years. Compared with them, this article introduces that the material N/O-HC-2 ranks among the top in terms of performance. In summary, the performance of the material has been greatly improved due to the introduction of nitrogen and oxygen double atoms, which increases more sodium storage sites and enhances conductivity.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Formation and Working Mechanisms</h3><div class="NLM_p">The mechanism of using the ball-milling pyrolysis process for C-HC modification is as follows. First, ball milling thoroughly mixes C-HC and urea and reduces their particle size, which promotes the pyrolysis reaction and increases the specific surface area. Second, the O 1s content of N/O-HC-2 (12.57%) is higher than that of C-HC (4.48%), which is attributed to the decomposition of urea into NH<sub>3</sub> and the reaction of HOCN with the carbon skeleton caused by heating. Therefore, a large number of C═O functional groups are generated on the surface of microcrystals, as shown in red in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a. The slope capacity of N/O-HC-2 is significantly higher than that of C-HC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b,c), and the analysis reasons are as follows: (1) the reaction between oxygen-containing functional groups and Na<sup>+</sup> contributes to the slope capacity, C═O + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>–</sup> → C–O–Na. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> (2) The generation of defects can provide slope capacity by adsorbing Na<sup>+</sup> through the pseudocapacitive effect. Defects arise from the formation of pyridine nitrogen; when a pyridine nitrogen is generated, the carbon framework is destroyed, resulting in a defect, as shown by the blue-green atom in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a. It is worth noting that one pyridinic N contributes one pπ electron to the graphitic π system, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> enhancing the electrical conductivity of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref45" aria-label="reference 29 and 45">(29,45)</a> This precisely explains the improved rate performance.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/medium/la4c02868_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Nitrogen doping mechanism of N/O-HC; schematic diagram of the sodium storage mechanism of (b) C-HC and (c) N/O-HC-2.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/asset/images/large/la4c02868_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The increase in platform capacity is attributed to the following reasons: (1) the modified N/O-HC-2 has an extended interlayer spacing, which is beneficial for the Na<sup>+</sup> storage contribution platform capacity. The lone-pair electrons of pyrrole nitrogen, perpendicular to the graphene plane, exhibit greater electrostatic repulsion and expand the interlayer spacing. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46−48)</a> The increase in interlayer spacing in carbon materials can reduce the hindrance to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). (2) Closed pores increase, and the number of closed pores has been proven to increase platform capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 33 and 49 to 52">(33,49−52)</a> Raman spectroscopy shows a higher degree of disorder in N/O-HC-2, which leads to more closed pores in the domain stacking for storing Na<sup>+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Based on the above analysis, it can be concluded that ball milling contributes to sodium storage by generating a small number of active sites to increase the slope capacity, successfully doping nitrogen and oxygen during secondary heating and subsequently introducing defects and oxygen-containing functional groups and increasing interlayer spacing to increase sodium storage capacity. In summary, this work established a three-dimensional network of modification strategies, material microstructures, and sodium storage mechanisms through various phase characterizations and electrochemistry.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have developed a process of codoping N and O to improve the electrochemical performance of coconut-shell-derived HC and explained its role and formation mechanism. The introduction of nitrogen and oxygen can regulate the microcrystalline surface, defects, interlayer spacing, and pore structure of HC. In the end, we found that the N/O-HC-2 sample with an oxygen content of 12.57% and a nitrogen content of 4.48% exhibited the best performance. Using cheap urea as the N/O source, N/O doping and a short-range disordered porous structure were achieved at a temperature of only 300 °C. The synthesized N/O-HC sample exhibits excellent electrochemical performance. The porous structure endows N/O-HC samples with abundant Na<sup>+</sup> storage active sites, effective diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>, and high specific surface area, resulting in sufficient contact between the electrolyte and carbon materials. The excellent electrochemical performance of N/O-HC is attributed to the synergistic effect of introducing oxygen and nitrogen, which generates abundant C═O groups, defects, extended <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub> spacing, and closed pore combinations. The optimized microstructure results in a first-time charging capacity of 343 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which is significantly improved compared to that of C-HC (272 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), and exhibits excellent rate performance, achieving 178 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Finally, benefiting from the economy of a urea nitrogen/oxygen source, N/O-HC provides a low-cost solution for the widespread commercialization of carbon-based anodes in SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Raman data, pore size analysis of all samples, XPS images/tables, electrochemical test images, and supplementary performance comparison data table mentioned in the main text (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868/suppl_file/la4c02868_si_001.pdf">la4c02868_si_001.pdf (749.41 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.langmuir.4c02868" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Dual-Function Presodiation with Sodium Diphenyl Ketone towards Ultra-stable Hard Carbon Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Hengyi; Gao, Suning; Ren, Meng; Huang, Yaohui; Cheng, Fangyi; Chen, Jun; Li, Fujun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">62</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">e202214717</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon (HC) is a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries, yet still suffers from low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).  Herein, sodium di-Ph ketone (Na-DK) is applied to realize dual-function presodiation for HC anodes.  It compensates the irreversible Na uptake at the oxygen-contg. functional groups and reacts with carbon defects of five/seven-membered rings for quasi-metallic sodium in HC.  The as-formed sodium induces robust NaF-rich SEI on HC in 1.0 M NaPF6 in diglyme, favoring the interfacial reaction kinetics and stable Na+ insertion and extn.  This renders the presodiated HC (pHC) with high ICE of ≈100% and capacity retention of 82.4% after 6800 cycles.  It is demonstrated to couple with Na3V2(PO4)3 cathodes in full cells to show high capacity retention of ≈100% after 700 cycles.  This work provides in-depth understanding of chem. presodiation and a new strategy for highly stable sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr0Tceqn3Pw1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhHlxfxoMQxaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XjtVOku7%252FI&amp;md5=141d49f7782b595cc2639b60c0618468</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803260"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ke, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Defect-Rich Soft Carbon Porous Nanosheets for Fast and High-Capacity Sodium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium ion storage in reduced graphene oxide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kumar, Nanjundan Ashok; Gaddam, Rohit Ranganathan; Varanasi, Srinivasa Rao; Yang, Dongfang; Bhatia, Suresh K.; Zhao, X. S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">214</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The performance of few-layered metal-reduced graphene oxide (RGO) as a neg. electrode material in Na-ion battery was studied.  Exptl. and simulation results indicated that the as-prepd. RGO with a large interlayer spacing and disordered structure enabled significant Na-ion storage, leading to a high discharge capacity.  The strong surface driven interactions between Na ions and O-contg. groups and/or defect sites led to a high rate performance and cycling stability.  The RGO anode delivered a discharge capacity of 272 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1, a good cycling stability over 300 cycles and a superior rate capability.  The present work provides new insights into optimizing RGOs for high-performance and low-cost Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr45N44-I1Dc7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhHlxfxoMQxaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsVyjsL%252FN&amp;md5=d4a37e837865e6c6707102c0b93f478e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902485"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bresser, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Transition Metal Oxide Anodes for Electrochemical Energy Storage in Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">1902485</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Transition Metal Oxide Anodes for Electrochemical Energy Storage in Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Shan; Bresser, Dominic; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1902485</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with outstanding energy and power d. have been extensively investigated in recent years, rendering them the most suitable energy storage technol. for application in emerging markets such as elec. vehicles and stationary storage.  More recently, sodium, one of the most abundant elements on earth, exhibiting similar physicochem. properties as lithium, has been gaining increasing attention for the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) in order to address the concern about Li availability and cost-esp. with regard to stationary applications for which size and vol. of the battery are of less importance.  Compared with traditional intercalation reactions, conversion reaction-based transition metal oxides (TMOs) are prospective anode materials for rechargeable batteries thanks to their low cost and high gravimetric specific capacities.  In this review, the recent progress and remaining challenges of conversion reactions for LIBs and SIBs are discussed, covering an overview about the different synthesis methods, morphol. characteristics, as well as their electrochem. performance.  Potential future research directions and a perspective toward the practical application of TMOs for electrochem. energy storage are also provided.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqAvr0rGrPgY7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhHlxfxoMQxaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitFGqu7bL&amp;md5=7093cb0d8eb5176859955db6a466ecbc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201700986"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xue, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">FeO<i>x</i>-Based Materials for Electrochemical Energy Storage</span>. <i>Advanced Science</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1700986</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-org. batteries, which use org. materials as the electrodes in Na-ion batteries, are an attractive alternative to conventional Li-ion batteries for next-generation sustainable and versatile energy storage devices owing to the abundant Na resources and environmental friendly features.  However, orgs. used in Na-ion batteries also encounter some issues such as low redox potential, high soly. in the electrolyte, and low cond.  In response, altering the arom. system/attaching electron-withdrawing groups, constructing polymers, and incorporating a conductive matrix are effective strategies.  This review summarizes and briefly discusses recent org. carbonyl compds. for Na-org. batteries from the viewpoint of function-oriented design, including function evolution from small-mol. compds. to polymers, then composites, and finally flexible electrodes.  In particular, as a timely overview, carbonyl-based org. flexible electrodes for Na-org. batteries are also highlighted for the 1st time.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoPzFwofQho5rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgF8LOs6du_tA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXit1OqtLjF&amp;md5=741f1c8f5584e73ac845a8b1e87d425d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.3390/ma11122567"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Whittaker, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are promising alternative electrochem. energy storage devices due to the abundance of sodium resources.  One of the challenges currently hindering the development of the sodium-ion battery technol. is the lack of electrode materials suitable for reversibly storing/releasing sodium ions for a sufficiently long lifetime.  Redox-active polymers provide opportunities for developing advanced electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries because of their structural diversity and flexibility, surface functionalities and tenability, and low cost.  This review provides a short yet concise summary of recent developments in polymer electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  Challenges facing polymer electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries are identified and analyzed.  Strategies for improving polymer electrochem. performance are discussed.  Future research perspectives in this important field are projected.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmomLSMHpKl7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgF8LOs6du_tA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitVylu7%252FE&amp;md5=1c56354fe540bdbf6cb3bcb6bcb0e153</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA04877C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A waste biomass derived hard carbon as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A waste biomass derived hard carbon as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Pin; Li, Yunming; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Li, Hong; Chen, Liquan; Huang, Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">34</span>),
